<h1 class="bookTitle" id="bookTitle" itemprop="name">
      The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe
      <a class="greyText" href="/series/49073-the-chronicles-of-narnia-publication-order">
          (The Chronicles of Narnia (Publication Order) #1)

<div class="readable stacked" id="description" style="right:0">
<span id="freeTextContainer12299309585541847553">When Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy took their first steps into the world behind the magic wardrobe, little do they realise what adventures are about to unfold. And as the story of Narnia begins to unfold, so to does a classic tale that has enchanted readers of all ages for over half a century.<p>This stunning version of the classic <em>The Lion, The Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, illust</p></span>
<span id="freeText12299309585541847553" style="display:none">When Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy took their first steps into the world behind the magic wardrobe, little do they realise what adventures are about to unfold. And as the story of Narnia begins to unfold, so to does a classic tale that has enchanted readers of all ages for over half a century.<p>This stunning version of the classic <em>The Lion, The Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, illustrated by Christian Burningham, comes with a special recording of the story which features a full production and specially composed music which transports the listener straight to the heart of Narnia. An absolute must for Narnia fans, and an excellent way of introducing the magical story to a new generation of readers. -<br><br>-<em>Susan Harrison</em></br></br></p></span>

<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4445815-richard"><img alt="" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/100x100/crop/users/1373539405/4445815.jpg"/></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/4445815-richard">Richard</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">C. S. Lewis was in the process of organising the stories into chronological sequence but died before he could make his intended changes to cause the<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">C. S. Lewis was in the process of organising the stories into chronological sequence but died before he could make his intended changes to cause the series to read more smoothly. As they stand now, if read in order of Narnian chronology one notices some difficulties in tone. Jadis, a heroic Amazonian figure in "The Magician's Nephew" shrinks into a mean-spirited witch in "The Lion, The Witch, and The Wardrobe". The tone of the various volumes varies considerably. As he wrote Lewis became more emotionally involved with his world and the writing became more profound. But a gain in reading in chronological order is an awareness of a cosmology beginning with Creation and ending with an Apocalypse. <br><br>On the other hand, if read in publication order, one experiences something of the delight and  surprise that the original readers of the series had as the narrative spotlight illuminated the various Narnian scenes and characters from book to book. <br><br>Of course, it is always possible to read the series both ways. Perhaps experience the publication order first and then sample the effects of a chronological approach.  In the end it is certainly your call. :}<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></br></br></br></br></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerFooter">

            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland">uk-and-ireland</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer163172902" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer8675648439461436014">My greatest disappointment in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4000436">'The Screwtape Letters'</a> was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The most he said was that believers were suffused with a vague light that even shone in the cat. This book has the same flaw, though the cat is much bigger.<br><br>Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him great or impressive. Sure, he helps the kids, bu</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText8675648439461436014" style="display:none">My greatest disappointment in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4000436">'The Screwtape Letters'</a> was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The most he said was that believers were suffused with a vague light that even shone in the cat. This book has the same flaw, though the cat is much bigger.<br><br>Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him great or impressive. Sure, he helps the kids, but all that makes him is a plot facilitator. He also has his big Jesus moment, but that has the same problem as the original: if he already knows that there will be no lasting negative outcome, how much of a sacrifice is it, really?<br><br>But then, Aslan isn't based on the original fig-cursing, church-rejecting, rebel Jesus, but the whitewashed version. Like Mickey Mouse, Jesus started out as an oddball troublemaker with his fair share of personality, but becoming the smiling face of a multinational organization bent on world domination takes a lot out of a mascot, whether your magic castle is in California or Rome.<br><br>Such a visible figure must become universally appealing, universally friendly and loving, lest some subset of followers feel left out. And it's this 'Buddy Christ' tradition from which Aslan seems to spring. Devoid of insight, wisdom, or charm, Aslan is just here to do things that our protagonists can't do.<br><br>This also beggars the question: why didn't Aslan just take care of all this stuff long before the kids arrived? Why did all the animals and fairies and giants have to suffer the pain of an endless winter? Except for a vague mention of prophecy, there isn't any reason for Aslan to wait for the kids, since in the end, he does it all on his own, anyways.<br><br>The only thing the kids do is help run of the battle, but this is only necessary because Aslan is absent, and he's only absent because the kids screwed up, indicating that the entire thing would have gone much more smoothly if they had never been there in the first place.<br><br>In that regard, I have to say Lewis did an excellent job boiling down Christianity into a fable, and leaving the <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QRTS_yM89gE" rel="nofollow">problem of evil</a> completely intact. Some readers suggest that Aslan lets the queen take over to teach the kids a lesson, but is it really worthwhile to let all the inhabitants of a kingdom suffer a century of misery just to teach a few kids about the true meaning of friendship?<br><br>The villain is just as poorly-constructed, and seems less concerned with defeating her enemies than with being pointlessly capricious. She manages to trick one of the children, but instead of taking advantage of this fact, she immediately makes it clear that she tricked him. I mean, how did someone that incompetent take over in the first place?<br><br>Selectively stupid characters are silly and convenient, especially as villains, because this completely undermines their role as foil. It is impressive when characters overcome challenges, but not when challenges simply crumble before them. The children are lucky the Queen was more of a <a href="http://www.pitt.edu/~dash/fart.html#devil" rel="nofollow">fart-stealing Old Nick</a> than a Miltonian Satan, otherwise they never would have stood a chance.<br><br>It is interesting to look at how many Christian authors have tried to reconcile their faith with complex fairy mythologies; not that Christianity doesn't have its own <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_of_tobit" rel="nofollow">magical fairy tales</a>, but these other traditions are not exactly compatible. Dante has Virgil lead him through hell, the Buddha was <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barlaam_and_Josaphat" rel="nofollow">made into a saint</a>, holidays were given new meanings (even if they often kept <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%92ostre" rel="nofollow">old symbols and names</a>), and magical monsters were also given a place in the new faith.<br><br>In the Middle Ages, monks compiled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beastiary" rel="nofollow">'Bestiaries'</a>, which described the roles of dragons, unicorns, and real animals in Christian synbolism; there were even century-spanning debates about <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cynocephaly#Late_Antiquity" rel="nofollow">whether dog-headed men were descended from Adam</a>. These books were rarely accurate, but allowed Christian theology to adopt many stories and superstitions from earlier periods; for instance, the connection between unicorns and virginity or the belief that pelicans fed their own blood to their young, in imitation of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transubstantiation" rel="nofollow">communion</a>.<br><br>So Lewis' attempt to take myth and adapt it to a Christian cosmology is hardly new--there is a long and storied tradition explored throughout the Chivalric period and recognizable today in books like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43545.The_Once_and_Future_King">The Once and Future King</a>, but Lewis doesn't a very good job of reconciling these disparate mythologies.<br><br>Like most Protestants, Lewis' religion was a modern one, not magical and mystical, but reasonable and utilitarian. He did not draw on the elaborate, convoluted apocrypha of hallucinatory monsters and miracles that mystics obsess over, instead, he made a small, sane, reasonable magical world--which rather defeats the point. It is unfortunate that many of today's readers think of Lewis' writings as defining English fairy tales, since his late additions to the genre are not original, nor are they particularly well-executed examples.<br><br>Many authors have come to the genre with much more imagination, a deeper sense of wonder, and a more far-reaching exploration of magic. We have examples from <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/571197.Puck_of_Pook_s_Hill">Kipling</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24213.Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland_Through_the_Looking_Glass">Lewis Carroll</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14686">Dunsany</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13624.The_Worm_Ouroboros">Eddison</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/189746">Morris</a>, and even modern updates by <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Gaiman</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201">Clarke</a>. Lewis, like Tolkien, may be a well-known example, but both are rather short-sighted, and neither one achieves as much as the many talented authors who came before.<br><br>I'm not saying Lewis is bad, merely that he is unremarkable, and is hardly preeminent in fantasy, or even in children's fantasy. However, I do think his fundamental message is a bad one, even if he didn't realize he was creating it. <br><br>In all of his worlds, he takes the sorts of people he dislikes, defines them as 'evil', then sets himself apart from them. There is no attempt to comprehend or to come to mutual understanding. I cannot respect a book which encourages people to vilify what they don't understand and to call isolation righteous. If any worldview deserves the epithet of 'evil', it is the sort of willful, prideful, self-indulgent ignorance Lewis displays.<br><br><a href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html" rel="nofollow">My List of Suggested Fantasy Books</a></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="8675648439461436014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_21724571" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21724571" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1008237-manuel" title="Manuel"><img alt="Manuel" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216930773p2/1008237.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21724571?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 31, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1008237-manuel" title="Manuel">Manuel</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21724571" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer9024903434605799987">I loved this book.<br>It was first read to me in 4th grade. We would all come in from lunch and our teacher would read to us for about 30 minutes before we would start class.<br>     I remember this book because it wasnt read to us by Mrs Graham, but instead it would be read by Mr Goodwin, her long-haired, bearded, Birkenstock wearing teacher's aid. <br>     Over the next few weeks we were enthralled by this story, we couldnt wait for lunch period to be over so we could hear what was happening in this magic kingdom</br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText9024903434605799987" style="display:none">I loved this book.<br>It was first read to me in 4th grade. We would all come in from lunch and our teacher would read to us for about 30 minutes before we would start class.<br>     I remember this book because it wasnt read to us by Mrs Graham, but instead it would be read by Mr Goodwin, her long-haired, bearded, Birkenstock wearing teacher's aid. <br>     Over the next few weeks we were enthralled by this story, we couldnt wait for lunch period to be over so we could hear what was happening in this magic kingdom, called Narnia.<br>     From the begining we all identified with Lucy and her siblings. How was it possible that an English girl could transport herself to another place, simply by hiding in a wardrobe? And once through the wardrobe, there was this wonderful and friendly creature called a faun, Mr Tumnus. All this in only the first chapter.<br>     As the chapters progressed we got to know more about the siblings and the other creatures who inhabit Narnia.<br>     Some people critisize C.S Lewis for using too much Christian symbolism, but I was in 4th grade and to me this was the most wonderful and exciting book ever written for children. <br><br>     When Mr Goodwin finished the book. I instantly went to the library so I could read it myself. I was very proud this was the first book I read "without pictures". To my joy, I discovered there were other books about Narnia and I eventually read all of them too. Evenutually I discovered other wonderful places in other books and I continue to look for them today.<br>     I will always be grateful to Mr Goodwin, he started off by telling me about Narnia, but in the end, he introduced me to so much more through my on going love of books.<br>     Thank you Mr Goodwin, for everything.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9024903434605799987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_208664579" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/208664579" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" title="Jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423696859p2/6313408.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/208664579?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 11, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=personal-favourites">personal-favourites</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=favourite-series">favourite-series</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=children-s-literature">children-s-literature</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=childhood-book">childhood-book</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=c-s-lewis">c-s-lewis</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer208664579" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15258510012975609699"><br><strong>A Defence of C.S. Lewis...or a brief attempt at such</strong><br><br>Some thoughts recently crossed my mind <s>no doubt on their way down-town to relax</s> in regards to arguments one could offer as a defence of the Christian side of this novel. The main arguments against this novel as a 'Christian allegory' that I have heard are: 1)Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure 2)That C.S. Lewis 'preaches' a black and white morality. So I'm going to roughly address them from my perspective and hope it encourages some discussion.</br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText15258510012975609699" style="display:none"><br><strong>A Defence of C.S. Lewis...or a brief attempt at such</strong><br><br>Some thoughts recently crossed my mind <s>no doubt on their way down-town to relax</s> in regards to arguments one could offer as a defence of the Christian side of this novel. The main arguments against this novel as a 'Christian allegory' that I have heard are: 1)Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure 2)That C.S. Lewis 'preaches' a black and white morality. So I'm going to roughly address them from my perspective and hope it encourages some discussion.<br><br>1) I will agree that Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure. Firstly for Aslan to really represent Christ he would have to be true to the gospel story. In other words he would have to be god made into man come to die for all mankind. However as he only dies for the one traitor again it's not sticking true to the Biblical gospel that all have sinned and that Christ was needed as a sacrifice for that sin. If you take things too literally here, C.S. Lewis' novel doesn't make much that much sense theologically as a result. I'll explain where I am/was going with that in a moment.<br><br>2) I debate that C.S. Lewis preaches in his novel. Occasionally he can be a touch patronising but compared to many authors he rarely slips into such condescension. As for his morality I think you must understand it from the perspective of Christianity. Christianity is about black and white morality essentially: good versus evil, light vs. dark and truth vs. lies etc. It is also very grey in that Christianity is about life and the fact that no one is perfect, that everyone fits into that moral grey area. Of course I explain roughly and inadequately.<br><br>Ultimately I see that there is room to argue that C.S. Lewis does a poor job of writing an allegorical novel. However I see it as a very subtle novel that unlike others (for instance The Alchemist) does not build its story around expressing an ideology but rather incorporates an ideology into its storytelling. I think that if one wants to criticise this novel it should be for not properly showing the gospel rather than for 'preaching'. I know that I and many others enjoyed the story first before seeing the connection between it and the Biblical tales. I enjoyed it even more afterwards so, then again I could be a tad biased.<br><br><strong>Original Review</strong><br><br>To begin I must note that I grant this such a high rating due to the impact it had on my life. It to me is one novel that were I to pick the one novel that forged a love of books for me it would be The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe. Why? Because I can remember back about twelve years ago when I was homeschooled by my mother as a five year old. We wandered down during winter into the warm back room and she read the first Narnia book to us. The image of a red faun carrying parcels as he passed a growing lamppost would stick with me from that moment (as it stuck with C.S. Lewis). As I learned to read the Narnia books were the first novels I sunk my growing reading teeth into. And to this day I have read and re read the novels back to front (and maybe front to back).<br><br>The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe is a novel written for both children and adults. It contains highly allegorical elements as C.S.Lewis was a well-known apologetics writer. However he wrote that he did not write his novel as a pure allegory but as a story. And that is what The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe is, a story to be enjoyed by everyone. And although written in simple language the reader can quickly, concisely and easily imagine the world without the clumsy constraints of overused words. I personally cannot imagine a world without these novels.<br><br>Additional thoughts:<br><br>1. Just a question at last. And one with a highly philosophical twist to it. Why is it that people so readily condemn those books which are considered as moral tales? You'd think we could do with more morality in such a twisted and confused world regardless of accepting the belief systems. <br><br>2. I have heard many people describe the entire series as silly and far too preachy. I do not see it that way at all. Trust me if C.S.Lewis wanted to be preachy he would have written a lot more philosophy and less story. Yes I can see how some would call this silly but then I argue that they are missing the point. It's a fairytale type fantasy intended mainly for children (and for those children again as adults or for their parents perhaps). But I argue that as Lewis only wrote this story based on the story of the crucifixion in many ways that it was not intended as a preachy book. My question is that why is it that if I were to base a story along what some call the 'Christian myth' it is claimed as preaching while as if I were to base it on any other mythology or story it would be deemed as merely copying the themes of another mythology? Is this yet another example of doublethink?*<br><br>*See <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984" title="1984 by George Orwell">1984</a> (yes go and read it - you'll get what I mean)</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="15258510012975609699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/569996-aimee" title="Aimee"><img alt="Aimee" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1207265736p2/569996.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20522082?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 19, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/569996-aimee" title="Aimee">Aimee</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer20522082" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15472198628409468872">I just re-read this book and got so much more out of it than the first time.  The symbolism &amp; parallels to basic Christianity stuck out. <br>*turkish delight is our human nature, prone to addiction, selfishness and wrongdoing<br>*Peter said about Edmund, "We should go after him.  After all he is our brother." Even though he had just betrayed them and was causing grief they didn't mistreat or disown him. <br>*The very mention of Aslan's name caused certain positive feelings to come over them all  they didn'</br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText15472198628409468872" style="display:none">I just re-read this book and got so much more out of it than the first time.  The symbolism &amp; parallels to basic Christianity stuck out. <br>*turkish delight is our human nature, prone to addiction, selfishness and wrongdoing<br>*Peter said about Edmund, "We should go after him.  After all he is our brother." Even though he had just betrayed them and was causing grief they didn't mistreat or disown him. <br>*The very mention of Aslan's name caused certain positive feelings to come over them all  they didn't know why.  But it made Edmund feel guilty.<br>*After Ed was returned and his siblings saw him for the first time Aslan said, "Here is your brother and there's no need to talk about what's in the past."  They forgave their brother. Aslan neither excused him nor condemned him.<br>*They all knew better than to go into a wardrobe &amp; shut the door as the book mentions a whole bunch of times.  We regularly do things when we know better.<br>*The professor makes them think and questions their disbelief in Lucy's story.  This is something the movie totally leaves out.  "Who would you usually believe, Lucy or Edmund?" etc.  Edmund shows the worst side of human nature, to betray &amp; let others down.<br>*I love that Father Christmas comes giving gifts that represent the gifts &amp; talents we each have to help others with and to overcome evil with.<br>There's more but I have to go! Loved the book. And the movie.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_285654370" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1" title="Shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/285654370?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 04, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1" title="Shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/1551102.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780006716631">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=inner-child">inner-child</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=fantasy-fantastic">fantasy-fantastic</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=travel-books">travel-books</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer285654370" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer468138013382663649">The Role (bibli)call:<br><br>The big cuddly cat = Jesus. Strange that a lion should be chosen to represent the big man when Lions are notoriously aggressive, solitary carnivores who are more likely to eat any potential apostles than than teach or lead them.<br><br>The white witch = Satan or Eve the temptress depending on which side of the tree of knowledge you're most likely to be barking up. Famed for a monochrome wardrobe in the A/W line only. Like Alan Rickman in Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves, she has cancel</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText468138013382663649" style="display:none">The Role (bibli)call:<br><br>The big cuddly cat = Jesus. Strange that a lion should be chosen to represent the big man when Lions are notoriously aggressive, solitary carnivores who are more likely to eat any potential apostles than than teach or lead them.<br><br>The white witch = Satan or Eve the temptress depending on which side of the tree of knowledge you're most likely to be barking up. Famed for a monochrome wardrobe in the A/W line only. Like Alan Rickman in Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves, she has cancelled Christmas.<br><br>Edmund = Judas Iscariot. Judas has been proven to be a more astute bargain maker and walks off with 30 pieces of silver for his denials. Edmund gets a box of sweets. <br><br>Lucy, Peter, Susan = apostles, knights and other positive biblical forces. An unusual scenario given the general hoo-hah about whether or not any of apostles were female (see last supper male/female image debate).<br><br>Mr Tumnus the faun = an aberration. With his goat like legs and general caprine features you might be forgiven for imagining that he might be an agent of Satan, or Pan or some other pagan deity. Nope. He's on the side of good and not evil and that there throws the nice set of biblical allusions into chaos.<br><br>Beavers, birds, satyrs, fauns and other ancillary creatures  = collateral damage.<br><br>Plot summary: Icing sugar, picture perfect winter wonderland accessible through the rear of roomy wardrobe handily equipped with high-end (but non PETA approved) all weather garb. Ruled in supremely effective manner by single minded, highly organised, independent woman until arrival of children and large pet. Maybe this book is actually a metaphor for home life in the modern age.<br><br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="468138013382663649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_331284223" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331284223" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2458503-choco" title="Choco"><img alt="Choco" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394302872p2/2458503.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331284223?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 06, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2458503-choco" title="Choco">Choco</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/170609.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780064404990">review of another edition</a>
<div>
<span class="greyText">Recommended to Choco by: </span>
      it's for class
  </div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=could-ve-been-better">could-ve-been-better</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=dissappointed">dissappointed</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=fer-class">fer-class</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=i-was-entertained">i-was-entertained</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=love-love-the-female-character">love-love-the-female-character</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=okay">okay</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=oooooh-the-cover-so-beautiful">oooooh-the-cover-so-beautiful</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458503-choco?shelf=very-interesting">very-interesting</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer331284223" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer12630593684243043454"><br>**SPOILERS**<br><br>You don't know how hard it was to sit and get started on this review. I already lost the first long one I wrote, and oh god, was it <em>long</em>. So just thinking of reviewing this again makes me really angry. <br><br>And you know what else makes me mad? I already wrote about most of what I mentioned in my lost review in an essay for <em>class</em>. So this is like writing all of that for the <em>third</em> time. Do you know how annoying that is? <br><br>Okay, let's quickly get into this.<br><img class="escapedImg" height="250" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380378008/733392.jpg" width="400"><br><br>I really liked the White Witch from t</br></br></img></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText12630593684243043454" style="display:none"><br>**SPOILERS**<br><br>You don't know how hard it was to sit and get started on this review. I already lost the first long one I wrote, and oh god, was it <em>long</em>. So just thinking of reviewing this again makes me really angry. <br><br>And you know what else makes me mad? I already wrote about most of what I mentioned in my lost review in an essay for <em>class</em>. So this is like writing all of that for the <em>third</em> time. Do you know how annoying that is? <br><br>Okay, let's quickly get into this.<br><img class="escapedImg" height="250" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380378008/733392.jpg" width="400"><br><br>I really liked the White Witch from the moment we first see her in the novel. I think my liking for her carried over from the movie – yes, I saw the movie first and I never read this as a kid – but for the most part, she was still pretty awesome in the book. <br><br>Well... sort of. Lewis kind of brags about Aslan a bit... or ... a lot... all the time, so the White Witch gets made fun of. I know some of you might say, well, it's a kids book, and for children at the time, they were  really careful on what went into the books and how frightening or sad it was. Lewis doesn't shut up about it either, he mentions it a few times. Like, oh, I would show you this, or I would tell you this, but if I did, believe me, you wouldn’t be allowed to read this book. <br><br>So... I understand that he might've felt the need to portray Aslan as very strong and mighty even when he was shown as weak, and to give the impression that everything is going to be okay – for the children, you know? But for me, and I'm not a kid, it's ... not so fun and gets boring because all the suspense is gone. <br><br>Maybe that's why I really liked the Witch. She brought with her a kind of ... evilness, and power, and she's a woman and she's ruling. I don't care if she's not supposed to, she's pretty awesome and she's feared. <br><br>In the movie, she seemed really, really proud to me, and I liked that. I kind of imagined her like that in the book too, but then Lewis gives us this: <br><em>"Haa-a-arrh!" roared Aslan, half rising from his throne; and his great mouth opened wider and wider and the roar grew louder and louder, and the Witch, after staring for a moment with her lips wide apart, <strong>picked up her skirts and fairly ran for her life.</strong> </em><br><br>No, please. She may not have her wand, but I don't think she would 'pick up her skirts and [run] for her life' – that's just not like her. Yeah, yeah, it's Lewis's character, and what do I know, but seriously people, authors always screw up their characters without noticing. I believe this is just  to show us that the Mighty Aslan is feared by all, and if that the Witch is scared of him, yay everything will be okay. <br><br>I also don't like the idea that she's shown as 'all bad' – like, she's entirely evil just because she's half jinn and half giant. (Yes, yes, it's a children's book, and the evil guy is just evil and needs to die – but let's ignore that for a few moments.)<br><br>I'd heard all about how this book is full of Christian themes and ideas, but 'jinn' are not Christian, are they? I don't think they are. <br><br>Let me tell you about jinn.<br><br>In Islam, there are humans, angels, and jinn. The humans were made of earth/clay/mud/whatever you prefer calling it, the angels were made of light, and the jinn were made of fire. <br>Out of the three creatures, only the angels are pure and are created to obey only. They do not, or <strong>
<em>cannot</em>
</strong> commit <em>any</em> sins. So there are no such things as fallen angels. Angels remain angels, and they are powerful creatures that serve god. <br><br>Both humans and jinn, however, have free will. They can choose either good are bad, and they have both good and evil inside them. Satan, the devil, is a jinn. He was thrown down to Earth because he defied god, and his promise to him was that by the time the Day of Judgement arrives, he'll lead as many people in to hell as he can. <br><br>In <em>A Thousand and One Nights</em>, or <em>Arabian Nights</em> – whichever version you know of, jinns are portrayed as both good and evil. The good ones are called 'faeries/fairies' I think, in the English translation. I don't know what the real Arabic version calls them, but if I ever get my hands on it, I'll let you know. Anyways, the bad jinn are called just... jinn, I think, and they are the followers of Iblis – which is just the name of the Devil. <br><br>What I'm trying to say is, that just like humans, the jinn have both good and bad. They can be evil, and they can be good. So when the Beavers tell the children that the White Witch is <em>all bad</em> and has no good in her because she's half jinn and half giant, being the daughter of Lilith, my reaction was, “Excuse me?” <br><br>There is no Lilith in Islam, and I'm not very sure about giants – though I do think they appear in <em>A Thousand and One Nights</em>, but they might be some way associated to jinn? -- but if she's part jinn then she has SOME GOOD IN HER DAMMIT. <br><br>Lewis burrowed all sorts of things with his giants and tree spirits and all of that, but come on. Why would you just take this 'jinn' and insert it into your story, just because? I know as a writer he's free to take whatever he wants, but that just doesn't make sense at all if we're looking at it from how Islam views jinn. <br><br>Then there's her name. The <em>White</em> Witch. Why is she the <em>White</em> Witch? If she is really all bad, why pick the color white? Isn't that 'pureness, innocence, clean' and a whole bunch of other 'good' things in the West? I guess you can say it's because the winter, or the ice, but hey, there are other colors too. There's blue, there's gray – or he could just not go with color at all! He could call her the Ice Witch, the Winter Witch, the Snow Witch – I don't know!<br><br>We talked about this in class too. Like, why white? So I said, well, she's half jinn. That was when I didn't know that there were no such things as jinn in Christianity. And they were like, is jinn white? <br><br>I don't really know, but if I were to pick a color for jinn, it would be white. I even went home and asked my mother, and she answered 'white' automatically, without even thinking about it. Maybe it's because in Islam, jinn are beings that live among our world, but mind their business if you mind your business. Maybe think of them as spirits, or ghosts (hate to call them ghosts but just for now). They're invisible to you though, so white just... makes sense. Maybe it's some influence of Eastern culture on me that I'm not aware of... but yes, a lady jinn... white fits her. <br><br>So I just cannot see her as someone who is entirely bad or evil. She's half jinn, she's given the color white, and to me, this woman had some reason for doing what she's doing. I know in the story, it's definitely NO WHAT THE FUCK ARE YOU TALKING ABOUT DID YOU EVEN READ but could Lewis have done a better job with her?<br><br>Uh, yeah. <br>Come on, jinn are <em>fascinating</em>. <br><br>You know what, I think I'll write something about jinn someday. Someday. <br><br>I swear I had more to say about her... but moving on.<br><br>Aslan. <br><br>His character is okay till around the part he gets sacrificed. <br>Oh, wait. No, that's not it.<br>It's when he gets reborn.<br>No...<br>That's not it either. <br><br>Oh yeah. <br>It's when he starts chasing the girls and goes yaaaay I'm a kitty come play with me tickle  me can I hug you yaaaaaayyy – oh shit. Girls, we've got a war going on, and we have some saving to do. Stop fooling around and get on my back, we gonna go rescuing. <br><br>Ah... hah... ha...<br><br>No, seriously. Does anyone understand what happened there? I think Lewis was trying to make Aslan NOT seem like the Christ figure, so he inserted that silly and completely out of character scene in there. I mean, he gets sacrificed for protecting a boy, for the greater good, don't you see him as a Christ Figure?<br><br>It's like Lewis went, oh crap. This dude's a lion, everyone's going to talk about this now and people would hate me for it.<br><br>Or I should ask, is it okay to compare animals to humans? I don't really know much about Christianity, so I guess I can't talk much about that.<br><br>But Aslan, buddy, when you roared everyone ran the fuck away, why are you suddenly acting like a little baby? It might've worked a little better if we saw Aslan acting more... childlike with the arrival of the kids, so suddenly coming back to life and playing around a bit would have been there in his character. <br><br>Wait, wait, actually, no. No. Even if you say like, oh, it's like a whole rebirth thing, and he's all young and full of energy now – do you not realize that <em>there's a war going on?</em> They just fool around for like, <em>hours</em>, and suddenly it's all business.<br><br>That makes NO sense. If Lewis had shown him as a more happy/fun loving character with the kids before, it still would've been really, really stupid of him to waste hours just chasing around the girls. And if you say, well, when he played around, he was RIGHT on time for everything else that happened -- still, no. It's a book. You can write it a billion ways and make it work -- but this does not work. <br><br>No wonder they cut that out in the movie. It's completely stupid. <br><br>Okay, finally to Susan. <br><br>I think I mentioned something about the essay I had to write when I was reading this book. So that essay turned into a comparison between this and <em>Alice's Adventures in Wonderland.</em> It was about culture, war, society, and... something. I don't know. But I talked a bit about why this or that was not included, so I'll skip most of that here. There's one important question left though:<br><br>Why was Susan given a freaking bow and arrow? <br>Why was Lucy given a knife?<br>Why was Susan, who was older than Edmund, not participating in the war with her freaking amazing bow which NEVER MISSES? <br>Why would you give someone something that they never end up using?<br>Why would you send the younger child to war? Why not send two of the older ones, and leave two of the younger ones to get a little closer? Edmund treated Lucy like shit, don't you think if Edmund and Lucy spent time together during that Aslan-is-getting-sacrificed bit, they'd get to know each other better? More bonding time! Or is it because one's a guy, and the other a girl, they wouldn't like to be in each other's company?<br><br><s>ohthat'snotONEquestionsorry</s><br><br>What is the reason for Edmund being such an ass? <br><br>I think the movie did a better job with this. If I remember right, he was all grouchy and mean because he missed his daddy--or both his parents? I'm not sure, but because of the war, that makes a ton of sense.<br><br>So why does Susan have such an amazing weapon if SHE DOES NOT USE IT? She uses it in the movie, so that was good. <br><br>But really, you have something that will strike anything you aim for, and... you don't use it? It just makes no sense. Why is that amazing weapon in this book?<br><br>Oh, but this is only one book, what do I know about the rest of the series? <br><br>I asked! AND THEY TOLD ME SHE DOESN'T DO ANY ARCHERY AND SHE DOESN'T KICK ANY ASS.<br><br>And you know, that's okay. It would be fine if Susan and Lucy were both shown as the characters who were more ... caretakers, supporters, helpers instead of fighters, but... <em>why giver her the freaking bow?</em><br><br>What's that there for? Lucy is given a dagger, and told to use it only when she really needs to. A little kid given a dagger, in a time of war, told it's dangerous and <em>only</em> to use when she <em>has</em> to. All right, I understand. She doesn't use it, and I understand. She didn't need to. Instead that potion... healing thing was more important.<br><br>But SUSAN. She's given a horn to blow when she needs help, and she's given an amazing freaking weapon. She uses the horn, but not that amazing weapon.<br><br>I feel sorry for that weapon. I really do. It's like if I buy chocolate, and I never eat it. What a waste. <br><br>Think about it a little though. If Susan had practiced a bit, she might've even saved Aslan from getting sacrificed. Maybe she could've hid in the shadows and fired an arrow at the White Witch or something. Oh, but there was no need, you say? Aslan was fine? <br><br>So? Are you just going to ignore the fact that one of the characters has an amazing weapon, just so it doesn't mess with your plot? And think about how awesome she would've been if she <em>did</em> take part in the war! Just standing on some hill, firing arrows right and left, hitting everything and everyone. <br><br>Imagine it for a second.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></img></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="12630593684243043454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<div class="review" id="review_929524361" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/929524361" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22313662-laz" title="Laz"><img alt="Laz" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423410628p2/22313662.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/929524361?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 27, 2014</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/22313662-laz" title="Laz">Laz</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/822603.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780007115617">review of another edition</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">everyone, really</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer929524361" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer3387042900040582538">Well, can you blame me for loving this? I certainly hope not. It's Christmas and I feel like a little kid and I was craving something to make me feel like I am one, indeed, and this book travelled me to a wondrous world full of heroes and of course a villain. The ride was awesome and I found the characters warm and fuzzy despite the eternal cold that had been placed upon Narnia. Now, they're all free of the curse of the White Witch thanks to Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy. The kings and queens of</span>
<span id="freeText3387042900040582538" style="display:none">Well, can you blame me for loving this? I certainly hope not. It's Christmas and I feel like a little kid and I was craving something to make me feel like I am one, indeed, and this book travelled me to a wondrous world full of heroes and of course a villain. The ride was awesome and I found the characters warm and fuzzy despite the eternal cold that had been placed upon Narnia. Now, they're all free of the curse of the White Witch thanks to Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy. The kings and queens of Narnia.<br><br><strong>
<blockquote>“Once a king or queen of Narnia, always a king or queen of Narnia.”</blockquote>
</strong><br><br>This is remarkable. It's amazing how short it is, I only read it in one and a half hour, I simply devoured it. It's short but so detailed and concentrated. It's like a fairytale. I'll certainly be reading this to my kids in the future. <br><br><strong>
<blockquote>“I wrote this story for you, but when I began it I had not realized that girls grow quicker than books. As a result you are already too old for fairy tales, and by the time it is printed and bound you will be older still. But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again. You can then take it down from some upper shelf, dust it, and tell me what you think of it. I shall probably be too deaf to hear, and too old to understand a word you say, but I shall still be your <br><br>affectionate Godfather, C. S. Lewis.”</br></br></blockquote>
</strong><br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="3387042900040582538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_350777668" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350777668" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5486168-dyuti" title="Dyuti"><img alt="Dyuti" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351864861p2/5486168.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/350777668?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 19, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5486168-dyuti" title="Dyuti">Dyuti</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">All fantasy lovers</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5486168-dyuti?shelf=children">children</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5486168-dyuti?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer350777668" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer5221617668087052159"><strong>What an amazingly delightful book! I regret not reading it earlier</strong><br><br><em> ** Before I begin, let me clarify that as I am not a Christian, I had no idea that this book was written by keeping the image of Jesus (as Aslan) in mind. I just came across this revelation on Goodreads, and it just added another layer to the story! The review below is written only by treating it as a fun-filled and action packed novel!</em><br><br><img alt="narnia" class="escapedImg" height="350" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380381163/738356.jpg" width="400"><br><br><br><br><br>I had seen the movie based on the book a couple of years ago, and had really loved it! On readi</br></br></br></br></br></img></br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText5221617668087052159" style="display:none"><strong>What an amazingly delightful book! I regret not reading it earlier</strong><br><br><em> ** Before I begin, let me clarify that as I am not a Christian, I had no idea that this book was written by keeping the image of Jesus (as Aslan) in mind. I just came across this revelation on Goodreads, and it just added another layer to the story! The review below is written only by treating it as a fun-filled and action packed novel!</em><br><br><img alt="narnia" class="escapedImg" height="350" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380381163/738356.jpg" width="400"><br><br><br><br><br>I had seen the movie based on the book a couple of years ago, and had really loved it! On reading the book, I realized it's just an extention of the movie(Should have been the other way round, but then, the one which comes first usually forms the greater impression). I'm glad the scripwriters did not destroy the essence of the story, which is (though simple), a charming tale!<br><br>Anyway, coming back to the story, it is about four adventurous children, who accidentally discover the magical world of Narnia behind a wardrobe in an old strange house. But all in not well in this magical land! It's under the reign of the evil queen -- the WHite Witch! Together with the help of a Magnificant Lion -- Aslan, the children go on to kill the witch and themselves become the Four Kings(and Queens!) of Narnia.<br><br>Though lacking much plot twists, I loved it simply because it was so easy-flowing! I altually wished I had a naughty kid near me whom I could catch hold of and read the book aloud to! I would love to see his wide-eyed amazement when Lucy would open the wardrobe for the first time, see his dissapointment when Edmund would betray them, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[hear his sigh when Aslan would sacrifice his life, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and catch that gleeful smile when they would win the Battle!<br><br>Maybe someday, sometime!<br><br>P.S: I really liked the dedication. <br><br>It read:<br><br><em> " My dear Lucy,<br>I wrote this story for you, but when I began it I had not realized that girls grow quicker than books. As a result you are already too old for fairy tales, and by the time it is printed and bound you will be older still. But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again. You can then take it down from some upper shelf, dust it, and tell me what you think of it. I shall probably be too deaf to hear, and too old to understand, a word you say, but I shall still be,<br>your affectionate Godfather,<br><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=C.S.Lewis" title="C.S.Lewis">C.S.Lewis</a>."</br></br></br></em><br><br><strong>
<em> Dear God, I hope I'm NEVER too old for fairy tales! </em>
</strong><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></img></br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_25953366" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25953366" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1166992-jess" title="Jess"><img alt="Jess" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1418433570p2/1166992.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25953366?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 28, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1166992-jess" title="Jess">Jess</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/1045154.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Hardcover 9780060234812">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=series">series</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jess?shelf=own">own</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25953366" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15016324199209426959">Every time I read this wonderful story, it's like catching up with an old friend. I've read this particular Narnia book so many different times, but it never ever gets old. I love the fact that I can pick up this (or any of the other Narnia books, for that matter) and step into a whole other world. I also particularly enjoyed the fact that I found that I could relate with each one of the children, although I must say that I was partial to Lucy! Additionally, I love the role Aslan plays in this b</span>
<span id="freeText15016324199209426959" style="display:none">Every time I read this wonderful story, it's like catching up with an old friend. I've read this particular Narnia book so many different times, but it never ever gets old. I love the fact that I can pick up this (or any of the other Narnia books, for that matter) and step into a whole other world. I also particularly enjoyed the fact that I found that I could relate with each one of the children, although I must say that I was partial to Lucy! Additionally, I love the role Aslan plays in this book because I think the ultimate sacrifice that he chooses to make shows how innately good he is. Even though this book is an easy read for me now at 18, this is one of those kinds of stories that you can never grow too old for. I really liked the line C.S. Lewis wrote in his dedication: "But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again." Isn't that the truth? Overall, I'd definitely have to call this a classic that everyone should read at some point or another. </span>
<a data-text-id="15016324199209426959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeText7632369748679466998" style="display:none">I am tempted to give this book a zero but the idea of going through the wardrobe to another land is fantastic.  Everything else, however, is not fantastic, including:<br><br>The over-the-top Christian allegory.<br>The complete absence of dramatic tension - the characters are static and the conclusion is foregone.  There is nothing to keep you reading, to challenge you, or to even vaguely interest you.<br>The writing is mediocre at best.<br>The dialogue is mediocre at best.<br><br>Awful book, it as if someone read Matthew through John, and then said these four gospels are good but it would take a master writer to retell them with talking animals and have it be worse to the point of complete boredom.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>

<span id="freeText8231656864653830758" style="display:none">This is a book that was written for children, and here we have adults analyzing it as if the story was meant for them to criticize. This is a fairy tale, with dwarfs and witches and animals that can speak. You can either be enchanted if you let go of your practical mindset, or disenchanted if you approach it as a jaded adult that can only grasp reality and adult situations. Obviously,  C.S. Lewis is not your thing, and I am not going to debate that, but I strongly disagree with you when you say that his writing is mediocre at best.  That's not even a matter of opinion in that case, is writing is far from mediocre, I am dumbfounded by that comment of yours!</span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_15886491" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15886491" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/904335-abi" title="Abi"><img alt="Abi" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1224713972p2/904335.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15886491?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 20, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/904335-abi" title="Abi">Abi</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/1551102.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780006716631">review of another edition</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">Smug Christians</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=children-s-literature">children-s-literature</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15886491" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer7127189951014593793">I read this when I was little (I would guess about 7, 8 or 9), and I didn't pick up on all the Christianity references, despite them being SO overt. What I did feel was astonishment that the children all loved Aslan so much, when I thought he was massively sanctimonious and sickening as a character. I could not <em>stand</em> that lion. I didn't want the Snow Queen to win exactly, I didn't like her either... but at the same time I wanted someone to show the lion up, or force him to do something INTERESTI</span>
<span id="freeText7127189951014593793" style="display:none">I read this when I was little (I would guess about 7, 8 or 9), and I didn't pick up on all the Christianity references, despite them being SO overt. What I did feel was astonishment that the children all loved Aslan so much, when I thought he was massively sanctimonious and sickening as a character. I could not <em>stand</em> that lion. I didn't want the Snow Queen to win exactly, I didn't like her either... but at the same time I wanted someone to show the lion up, or force him to do something INTERESTING that would show he wasn't an unmitigating goody-goody. Aslan was just... ugh, completely two dimensional in his perfection, and so smug and arrogant. He was <em>unbearable</em>. I enjoyed the fantasy, but the element that I now realise (and am astonished that I ever didn't realise) is the Christian allegory made me scornful of the character who was supposed to be the supreme 'goody' and left a bitter taste in my mouth that ultimately ruined it. I enjoyed other books in the series more, some more than others (enjoyment increased exponentially as Aslan's involvement decreased), but this one was the worst.</span>
<a data-text-id="7127189951014593793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer221362875" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer12588556320635122566">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by CS Lewis is one of the books in his series, the Chronicles of Narnia in which Christianity is portrayed through various fantasy creatures. God, for instance is portrayed as a talking Lion. What a wonderful series! What child hasn’t climbed into a closet and explored the back cracks in hope of finding an entrance to a new and exciting world after reading this book? I used to sit in a closet with the door closed and a flashlight reading my favorite books aft</span>
<span id="freeText12588556320635122566" style="display:none">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by CS Lewis is one of the books in his series, the Chronicles of Narnia in which Christianity is portrayed through various fantasy creatures. God, for instance is portrayed as a talking Lion. What a wonderful series! What child hasn’t climbed into a closet and explored the back cracks in hope of finding an entrance to a new and exciting world after reading this book? I used to sit in a closet with the door closed and a flashlight reading my favorite books after reading this series, in hopes that someday a door would open and take me to another realm. Of course, the white witch is my favorite character. I’m always attracted to the bad ones. The Lion, Aslan, is a wonderful character as well, but I have to admit, knowing that he was an analogy for God, changed my view of the story a bit and left me a bit disappointed. He was a bit cheesy. Or maybe typical is a better word. Which is why I almost wish I wouldn’t have known the true meaning of the books until after I read them. <br><br>In any case, the stories were great, the first one being the best. (You always lose a little of the naiveté of the children as they get older) But the movies did them justice as well. Reading them again as an adult, found me a little bored, but still enchanted overall with the series. The next movie is due out soon and I can only hope they will continue to make the movies which were incredible. I highly recommend this series and consider it a classic as well.<br><br>Note: I've recently started these again with my 5 yr old and we are loving it.  I get to see it again through a child's eyes.  And this time I haven't told him anything about the analogy to Christianity, however I do point out certain lessons such as forgiveness.  (When Aslan has his talk with Edmund)  I want him to figure it out on his own.  We had a popcorn movie night and watched the movie together curled up under a blanket a few days ago and as I sat there, I thought to myself, this is why I had children.  To experience all my favorite things over again through a child's eyes.  It was the best time.<br><br><a href="http://classicsdefined.com/narnia.html" rel="nofollow"> ClassicsDefined.com</a><br><br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="12588556320635122566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_18368388" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18368388" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner" title="Werner"><img alt="Werner" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18368388?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 04, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/903390-werner" title="Werner">Werner</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone (of any age) who loves fantasy, and/or serious Christian literature</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=classics">classics</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18368388" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4062573773647341050">While I first read this book in the mid-70s, I read it again to my wife a couple of years ago (we both loved it then as much as I did the first time).  Most people know that C. S. Lewis was an effective Christian nonfiction apologist, using the tools of reason and logic to build the philosophical case for Christian faith.  But he ultimately became convinced that an even more effective apologetic is available through the "truth of art," the instinctive and emotional appeal that stories exert -- esp</span>
<span id="freeText4062573773647341050" style="display:none">While I first read this book in the mid-70s, I read it again to my wife a couple of years ago (we both loved it then as much as I did the first time).  Most people know that C. S. Lewis was an effective Christian nonfiction apologist, using the tools of reason and logic to build the philosophical case for Christian faith.  But he ultimately became convinced that an even more effective apologetic is available through the "truth of art," the instinctive and emotional appeal that stories exert -- especially the kinds of stories that draw on the deep, mythical archetypes of fantasy to illuminate the real universe.  The Chronicles of Narnia, his classic fantasy series, was the fruit of that discovery, set in Narnia, a magical land whose world lies in another universe, in which magic works and time moves differently than it does here, and in which Christ is incarnate as the great talking lion Aslan.  This first book of the series presents one of the most powerful symbolic literary presentations of the Christian gospel ever written.  Although the intended audience, in Lewis' mind, was children (and his author's various direct addresses to the readers presuppose this), there is nothing invidiously "juvenile" about the quality of the writing; it can be enthusiastically appreciated by anyone who loves tales of imagination and adventure, fantasy and wonder; and the truths here, like those in Jesus' parables, are simple enough to speak to children but profound enough to challenge adults.<br><br>This is the "first" book of the series in the sense of first to be written (and usually the first to be read).  However, The Magicians Nephew is a prequel which describes the creation of Narnia, and the origins of the White Witch and of the wardrobe that serves as a gateway to Narnia; Lewis himself recommended that this prequel be read first.</br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="4062573773647341050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<a class="user" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff" title="Erik Graff">Erik Graff</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/317500.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780020442202">review of another edition</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">no one</span></div>
<div>
<span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      Skip and Flo
  </div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=literature">literature</a>
</div>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer35165950" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer11633415117715491171">During my freshman year at Grinnell College we had resident advisors, a couple who lived downstairs in Loose Hall dorm who were there if we needed them.  That never happened, but they were friendly and welcoming, their door usually open to visitors.  My roommate, Richard Hyde, had some relationship with them and invited me to join him for a visit.<br><br>Skip and Flo were Christians! That was intriguing.  I asked them about it and they gave me an answer which I only came to understand years later, after I'</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText11633415117715491171" style="display:none">During my freshman year at Grinnell College we had resident advisors, a couple who lived downstairs in Loose Hall dorm who were there if we needed them.  That never happened, but they were friendly and welcoming, their door usually open to visitors.  My roommate, Richard Hyde, had some relationship with them and invited me to join him for a visit.<br><br>Skip and Flo were Christians! That was intriguing.  I asked them about it and they gave me an answer which I only came to understand years later, after I'd read Schweitzer and Bultmann.  It basically amounted to their agreeing that, yes, Christianity was absurd and that the core of it was Christ crucified.  Not the resurrection.  That's absurd.  Christ crucified.  --At least, that's the way I constructively interpreted these nice people four years later when I actually had come to know quite a lot about their religion.<br><br>They also loaned me a copy of Lewis' children's book, The Lion etc., first volume of his Narnia series, presumably because I'd mentioned having read and liked his Perelandra Trilogy and autobiography.  <br><br>Dutifully, I gave it a try...and hated it.  Now, as an adult of sorts, having also read and despised his Screwtape Letters, I could see through the story to the underlying propaganda and it bothered me a lot that children were intended to be exposed to it.  Still, they were nice people.  I suffered through the thing out of respect for them.<br><br>Now, reflecting on my own early exposure to, and appreciation of, C.S. Lewis, I am less prone to be upset.  I survived the insidious brainwashing.  It appears my fourteen year old niece has managed to do so in recent years as well.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="11633415117715491171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_15377927" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15377927" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/784513-danielle" title="Danielle"><img alt="Danielle" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220889844p2/784513.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15377927?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 13, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/784513-danielle" title="Danielle">Danielle</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_2" title="it was ok">2 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/784513-danielle?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15377927" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer10232417444208850407">Okay, let me say first of all that I think C.S. Lewis is a brilliant man. That being said, this book made me feel like he could have given a little credit to the rest of us.<br>I wish I could review it based solely on the story, and not bother with the religious message, but unfortunately, it's impossible to read the story without getting a headache from the constant hammering that is "THE MESSAGE." <br>This story is a fun idea, fantasy-wise. I particularly love the ice queen and the Turkish Delight sce</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText10232417444208850407" style="display:none">Okay, let me say first of all that I think C.S. Lewis is a brilliant man. That being said, this book made me feel like he could have given a little credit to the rest of us.<br>I wish I could review it based solely on the story, and not bother with the religious message, but unfortunately, it's impossible to read the story without getting a headache from the constant hammering that is "THE MESSAGE." <br>This story is a fun idea, fantasy-wise. I particularly love the ice queen and the Turkish Delight scene with Edmund. However (and there is a big 'however'), I just couldn't get past the whole lectured-in-church feeling that this book produced. <br>Don't get me wrong, I am a religious person, and I believe in Christ with all my heart. But, I personally get a lot more out of reading scriptures about him than this allegorical fantasy. So, if Lewis had allowed it to be a layered thing, ya know, enjoy the story, and if you see the parellels to Christ and his mission, good for you, I would have enjoyed it a lot more. As it was, I felt like every other paragraph was practically screaming at me, "Get it?? You Get IT?? The LION, came back to LIFE!!" Yeah, we got it.<br>I read Prince Caspian because I was curious about the rest of the story, but I'm done. It was a little too much for me, and I'll just stick to my scriptures, rather than talking lions.</br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="10232417444208850407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>
