<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_563173459" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/563173459" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18438835-callum" title="Callum"><img alt="Callum" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1381804049p2/18438835.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/563173459?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 28, 2013</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/18438835-callum" title="Callum">Callum</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/317500.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780020442202">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18438835-callum?shelf=contemporary-fantasy">contemporary-fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18438835-callum?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18438835-callum?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18438835-callum?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18438835-callum?shelf=christianity">christianity</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer563173459" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer10454875202326488134">Regardless of the anyone's critical opinion, <em>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</em> is one of the most memorable pieces of fantastic literature ever produced (completely disregarding the pop culture influence). Its simple premise, reticulated with a vast allegorically fantastical world remains an incredibly effective method of storytelling, as its themes and concepts still resonate today.<br><br>The premise of the story is relatively straightforward; four young children are moved to the countryside to av</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText10454875202326488134" style="display:none">Regardless of the anyone's critical opinion, <em>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</em> is one of the most memorable pieces of fantastic literature ever produced (completely disregarding the pop culture influence). Its simple premise, reticulated with a vast allegorically fantastical world remains an incredibly effective method of storytelling, as its themes and concepts still resonate today.<br><br>The premise of the story is relatively straightforward; four young children are moved to the countryside to avoid a war torn London, and are arbitrarily <em>(or so it may seem)</em> entangled into the affairs of another world. However, this isn't just some escape for the young children. Ironically, in avoiding the conflict that afflicts their home, they become the key turning point in another, more elaborate struggle.<br><br>It progresses in a manner that leaves the reader wondering why they even bother assisting in this vast nearly improbable endeavour, but like any great story, it becomes so palpably apparent without resorting to brazenfaced exposition. His ideas are presented mysteriously, oftentimes are aspects of Narnia hinted or implied, even the grander aspects are enigmatic. They are influenced greatly by the Christian (and other mythological) allusions presented in the novel. <br><br>This struggle oftentimes showcases common Christian themes such as temptation, faith, betrayal and sacrifice; all of which are basically touted, but unequivocally pertinent to the story. They reveal both the strengths and weaknesses of the four children, their callowness and their ripening. These themes grasp  the cast of side characters as well, who are as distinguished as they are intriguing. They are not defined but developed by these themes.<br><br>Mr. Tumnus, perhaps woven from the story's most infamous image, typifies the challenges of these themes, as do both Aslan and Edmund. Some brush it off as brash allegory, but I see it as a clever, and an even more accessible envisioning of topics which were otherwise bombastic in the hands of overzealous vicars. They were presented as choices, not duties and still preserved the Christian virtue that Lewis still closely follows.   <br><br>So this novel isn't merely a simple piece of fantasy. As familiar themes and concepts are weaved, the story becomes ultimately original. And though C.S. Lewis is very much a religiously devout man, his allusions are presented more passionately than piously. He sprouts truth through fantasy, and at the same time creates an entertaining story.<br><br>Though the oddities of this quasi-fairytale novel are quite tame in comparison to Eddison or Carroll, I think The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe remains an effulgent presence in the genre. His tale of heroism, his contemporary style blended with a high fantasy splendour both succeeded in telling an entertaining story that didn't falter under heavy themes and concepts. It was both undone and unheard of at the time of its release. <br><br>Of course, The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe holds heavy influence on contemporary works today especially considering the blend of Earth-dwellers placed in high fantastical situations. Thankfully the novel isn't worn with age. Its immensity and broadly-thematic tale remain as rich and accessible as any contemporary fantasy work today. It also remains a model for works of this ilk. Lewis transcends the whims and expectations of contemporary fantasy, and the banality of heroic fantasy. <br><br>This, more than any other book, holds sentimental value. It was my ingress to fantasy (even preceding The Lord of the Rings and Harry Potter). And the best part is that this novel is more important to me now than ever, as my keenness on fantasy rises so does my reverence for The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe. The subtle aspects becomes clearer, as do the themes and the allegory. It is, irrevocably, an important piece of fantastic literature.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="10454875202326488134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer630428623720245072"><em>"Same here. It's a childhood love. It's nice to see someone write something positive about Narnia for a change! So many people shoot it down for its r</em></span>
<span id="freeText630428623720245072" style="display:none"><em>"Same here. It's a childhood love. It's nice to see someone write something positive about Narnia for a change! So many people shoot it down for its religious themes--I don't quite see why."</em><br><br>I was also surprised at the amount of people who disliked this novel. I guess the Christian Allegory was the most potent instigator but one must remember that the book is not simply revered for being an infamous Christian allegory. <br><br>I was around 8 or 9 when I read this novel and the Christian allegories flew over my head. When I found out the story was built on Christian allegory, I'll admit because it's built from a religion I don't follow, I was a bit flustered. But I realized the Christian allegory didn't really ruin the story. Neil Gaiman's views are quite similar to my own, as he says:<br><blockquote>"My upset was, I think, that it made less of Narnia for me, it made it less interesting a thing, less interesting a place. Still, the lessons of Narnia sank deep. Aslan telling the Tash worshippers that the prayers he had given to Tash were actually prayers to Him was something I believed then, and ultimately still believe."</blockquote><br><blockquote>"I think, perhaps, the genius of Lewis was that he made a world that was more real to me than the one I lived in; and if authors got to write the tales of Narnia, then I wanted to be an author."</blockquote><br><em>Lit Bug wrote: "I need to read this! "</em><br><br>It's a classic that should be read by anyone wanting to get into fantasy (whether you like it or dislike it, actually). And yes, it's children's lit. You'll probably notice a lot of Christian allegory, I would advise not ignoring it, and just make of it what you will. I for one loved how it's based into the story, and while C.S. Lewis isn't the most intellectual supporter of Christianity, no one can deny his passion.<br><br><em>"I saw the movies and especially liked the first in the series :)"</em><br><br>I saw the film as well, I don't plan on watching the sequels until I read the books first.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="review" id="review_64516099" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64516099" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111921-madeline" title="Madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64516099?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 22, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/111921-madeline" title="Madeline">Madeline</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=kids-and-young-adult">kids-and-young-adult</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=the-movie-is-better">the-movie-is-better</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer64516099" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer10351391913343359997">Rather than spend this review explaining what I thought of the book, I will instead devote my time to justifying placing this on my "the movie is better" shelf.<br><br>Reasons the Movie Version Is Better:<br>1. Watching it will take about the same amount of time it takes to read the book, because good lord does everything get wrapped up quick.<br>2. We actually get to see some real fight scenes, and even though there's no blood and it's all very PG, at least it's not just "There was a battle going on but then A</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText10351391913343359997" style="display:none">Rather than spend this review explaining what I thought of the book, I will instead devote my time to justifying placing this on my "the movie is better" shelf.<br><br>Reasons the Movie Version Is Better:<br>1. Watching it will take about the same amount of time it takes to read the book, because good lord does everything get wrapped up quick.<br>2. We actually get to see some real fight scenes, and even though there's no blood and it's all very PG, at least it's not just "There was a battle going on but then Aslan showed up and it was over yay!" <br>3. The dialogue is considerably less dated and irritating. The kids in the book speak like plummy old British men watching a cricket match in 1800: "'We've fallen on our feet and no mistake,' said Peter. 'This is going to be perfectly splendid. That old chap will let us do anything we like.'" <br>Meanwhile, the movie has lines like this: "When Adam's Flesh and Adam's bone sits at Cair Paravel in throne, the evil time will be over and done." "You know that doesn't really rhyme." <br>4. Aslan becomes much less of a dick when he's voiced by Liam Neeson. <br>5. Since the book is so short (see Reason 1) there's plenty of time for the film to do justice to everything in C.S. Lewis's material, and even add some things. For instance, did you know the Pevensie kids actually have a mother? With a <em>face</em>? Shocking, I know. <br>6. William Mosely is a pretty, pretty boy. Peter the Magnificent, indeed.<br><br>DON'T YOU JUDGE ME. <br><a href="http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0608440/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0608440/</a></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="10351391913343359997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1103494217" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer13870059476266030881"><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7806720.The_Lion__the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by C.S. Lewis">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</a> is an intelligent and entertaining story for children and adults alike. <br><br>This is another book I decided to read along with my son after he was asked to read it for school. As a student, it can be hard to get excited about books th</br></br></span>

In a fascinating world of magical wardrobes, talking animals, and mythical creatures, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7806720.The_Lion__the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by C.S. Lewis">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</a> is an intelligent and entertaining story for children and adults alike. <br><br>This is another book I decided to read along with my son after he was asked to read it for school. As a student, it can be hard to get excited about books that are "assigned" to you or are listed as "required" reading. One of the joys of reading is picking out exactly what you want whenever you want. But with <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7806720.The_Lion__the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by C.S. Lewis">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</a>, he just couldn't wait for the next day's chapters! <div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1062427423" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062427423" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" title="Wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423757686p2/9246169.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062427423?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 12, 2015</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" title="Wanda">Wanda</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=100-to-well-read">100-to-well-read</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=brit-lit">brit-lit</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1062427423" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer1279086632786001779">I’m pretty sure that I read this when I was 11 or 12—but I didn’t remember it at all.  In my defense, that was about 40 years ago.  Here’s the funny thing though—as a kid, if I liked a book, I re-read it numerous times.  So, if I did read it, lo those many years ago, I didn’t like it enough to re-read it.  Around 12 or 13, I also read <em>The Lord of the Rings</em>, for example, and I have read it innumerable times now and continue to enjoy it every once in a while.<br><br>Now, I’m not sure if I believe this premise</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText1279086632786001779" style="display:none">I’m pretty sure that I read this when I was 11 or 12—but I didn’t remember it at all.  In my defense, that was about 40 years ago.  Here’s the funny thing though—as a kid, if I liked a book, I re-read it numerous times.  So, if I did read it, lo those many years ago, I didn’t like it enough to re-read it.  Around 12 or 13, I also read <em>The Lord of the Rings</em>, for example, and I have read it innumerable times now and continue to enjoy it every once in a while.<br><br>Now, I’m not sure if I believe this premise or not, but in <em>The End of Your Life Book Club</em> by Will Schwalbe, his mother had a theory—we are all either Narnia fans or Middle Earth fans.  In her experience, people rarely loved both fantasy worlds.  She and one child were Narnia people and her son Will inhabited Middle Earth in a way that both of them envied.<br><br>Reading <em>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</em> now as an adult well into middle age, I find the Christian symbolism in it to be obvious and heavy handed.  (Mind you, if you had little exposure to Christianity, I’m sure many parts of the story line would be inexplicable!)  By contrast, although many people say they see similar things in Tolkien, I seem to be blind to it.  Yes, there is a struggle between good and evil, but it doesn’t seem to me to be a particularly Christian world or world view and <em>The Lord of the Rings</em> certainly doesn’t clobber you with Christianity the way than Lewis’ work does. (In fact, LOTR feels very pagan to me).<br><br>I guess this is my way of saying if the world really does split itself evenly into Team Narnia and Team Middle Earth, count me in as a member of Team Middle Earth.  Despite the fact that I quite enjoyed the fantasy adventure, the Greek mythological beings (fauns, centaurs, dryads, etc.), plus the references to Northern European mythology (the Witch seems to be to be a relative of the Snow Queen, and Father Christmas has become a regular everywhere).  Mind you, it also seems to me to be a bit unfair to compare definite children’s literature (Narnia) with a book that I began enjoying as a tween and that continues to comfort me now in my 5th decade (LOTR).<br><br>I certainly see why this book has become a classic and why it still appeals to children today.  <br><br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="1279086632786001779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/140212.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Hardcover 9780061125232">review of another edition</a>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer384149987" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4397490668879052889">Fantasy has over the years become one of my favourite genres - if it hasn't always been. Ever since that first encounter with C.S. Lewis.<br><br>I was young when my mother came home with the seven novels about Narnia. I was always an avid reader, wrote my first book at nine. I can't remember exactly what she told me of them, but I do remember my eyes. They were so large, I figure anyone who watched me might've thought they would jump out of their sockets. I read them as a homeless might gulp down food w</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText4397490668879052889" style="display:none">Fantasy has over the years become one of my favourite genres - if it hasn't always been. Ever since that first encounter with C.S. Lewis.<br><br>I was young when my mother came home with the seven novels about Narnia. I was always an avid reader, wrote my first book at nine. I can't remember exactly what she told me of them, but I do remember my eyes. They were so large, I figure anyone who watched me might've thought they would jump out of their sockets. I read them as a homeless might gulp down food when offered some. To follow the Pevensies through that door into Narnia was wondrous, and the mere idea of another world so close to ours striking.<br><br>The experience is unquestionably one of the most memorable of my childhood, and I will carefully storage my precious editions of the Chronicles of Narnia until the day can pass them on to my children. That day I will watch them, most joyously and serenely, as their eyes become as large as saucers and as they bite their nails in anticipation when the Pevensies yet again, for their sake, step through that door.</br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_64739123" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64739123" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4695-daniel" title="Daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264806364p2/4695.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64739123?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 28, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/4695-daniel" title="Daniel">Daniel</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/317500.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780020442202">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=2009">2009</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer64739123" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer7675931660766288099">Unlike some who re-read "The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe" as adults and bring their fond childhood memories to the experience, I barely remember reading it the first time, when I was quite young. So, reading it now, I'm judging it solely as an adult would. And while some children's books fare incredibly well when read by adults -- even those adults who never read them as children -- C.S. Lewis's book, I'm sorry to say, is not one of them.<br><br>"Lion" is not a bad book, to be sure, but its charact</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText7675931660766288099" style="display:none">Unlike some who re-read "The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe" as adults and bring their fond childhood memories to the experience, I barely remember reading it the first time, when I was quite young. So, reading it now, I'm judging it solely as an adult would. And while some children's books fare incredibly well when read by adults -- even those adults who never read them as children -- C.S. Lewis's book, I'm sorry to say, is not one of them.<br><br>"Lion" is not a bad book, to be sure, but its characters, especially the humans, are a bit bland to stick with the reader once the book is closed. The exception is Edmund Pevensie, who is memorable only because Lewis makes him so unrelentingly obnoxious for almost the entire book. Lewis also draws on myriad old myths, fables and legends to create the hodgepodge that is Narnia, creating little from scratch -- a criticism also leveled at J.K. Rowling decades later. The plot of "Lion" is a bit creaky, too, with some machinations making little sense on their own, and needed solely to keep the story moving forward. I'm thinking, for example, of the note left behind at Mr. Tumnus's house after he is arrested -- a note that exists only so that the Pevensie children can find out what became of the faun.<br><br>But some of the touches in Lewis's writing remain fresh almost 60 years after the book was written. Lewis never lets you forget he's telling you a story, occasionally interjecting his own opinions of the characters' doings, and more than once reminding the reader of something that happened in "the last chapter." Also, now and again, he directly addresses the reader as "you," as would a parent trying to draw a child into a story.<br><br>What else makes "Lion" intersting to the adult reader? Well, there is, late in the book, some hot girl-on-girl-on-lion action. Sure, C.S. Lewis mostly was writing a religious parable for children, but he threw in some thinly veiled steaminess for his adult readers too. Don't think I don't know what you were doing, Jack. I do.<br><br>Also, spoiler alert: Aslan is totally Jesus Christ.<br><br>OK, on to "Prince Caspian."</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="7675931660766288099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1330562381584603169">Good review. I too have problems with CS Lewis. He has a special rather uncomfortable resonance for me since "Narnia" was apparently based on the hill</span>
<span id="freeText1330562381584603169" style="display:none">Good review. I too have problems with CS Lewis. He has a special rather uncomfortable resonance for me since "Narnia" was apparently based on the hills that lie above our small town of Holywood in N Ireland. I suppose I don't share his theology (I'm not a Christian, but Lewis's Christianity seems to be decidedly peculiar) or his prejudices. Perhaps he is too much a man of the 1940s and 1950s which I still recall, and still dislike. Each to his (or her)own. </span>

<div class="review" id="review_1101089" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1101089" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76999-katie-lambden" title="Katie Lambden"><img alt="Katie Lambden" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1178646241p2/76999.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1101089?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">May 08, 2007</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/76999-katie-lambden" title="Katie Lambden">Katie Lambden</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_2" title="it was ok">2 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1101089" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer13290762158083622653">My mom read the entire Chronicles of Narnia as bedtime stories when I was about 8.  Of course at the time I perceived none of the allegorical "depth," but did have Turkish Delight fantasies galore.  After the movie release hoopla about CS Lewis last year, I decided to re-read at least one of the books.  Often I come back to childhood favorites and they're like comfort food; I could read them again and again.  However, I was not so taken with Narnia on my return visit.  The storytelling is not that am</span>
<span id="freeText13290762158083622653" style="display:none">My mom read the entire Chronicles of Narnia as bedtime stories when I was about 8.  Of course at the time I perceived none of the allegorical "depth," but did have Turkish Delight fantasies galore.  After the movie release hoopla about CS Lewis last year, I decided to re-read at least one of the books.  Often I come back to childhood favorites and they're like comfort food; I could read them again and again.  However, I was not so taken with Narnia on my return visit.  The storytelling is not that amazing, and the characters are frustratingly transparent vehicles for Lewis's real objective, which is the retelling of the Passion of the Christ.  Plus there's an excessive and annoying degree of anthropomorphizing (OK, so the animals must be able to talk in order for the story to go forward, but must the beavers wear snowboots and Aslan the lion stand on two feet?).<br>The central question for me, however, is how useful such an imaginative allegory can possibly be.  I guess CS Lewis can thumb his nose at me for this question, though, since people are still reading the Chronicles of Narnia and still making movies not only about Aslan but also human versions of the Passion of Christ.</br></span>
<a data-text-id="13290762158083622653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer264898080" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer9982837784338603343">The number of editions I have shelved of this book is going to become ridiculous. It's not that I'm reading it in different editions, there's just no way to record multiple read dates... Oh well.<br><br>I used to love this book the most of the whole series. It's magical, and Aslan is such a prominent part of it, and of course it's the book that makes every child check in their wardrobe hopefully. Actually, I still wish I could believe that -- one of my housemates has a most excellent deep wardrobe that</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText9982837784338603343" style="display:none">The number of editions I have shelved of this book is going to become ridiculous. It's not that I'm reading it in different editions, there's just no way to record multiple read dates... Oh well.<br><br>I used to love this book the most of the whole series. It's magical, and Aslan is such a prominent part of it, and of course it's the book that makes every child check in their wardrobe hopefully. Actually, I still wish I could believe that -- one of my housemates has a most excellent deep wardrobe that would be a very good passage to Narnia. Narnia still feels quite real to me, in this book, because the four kids are so very ordinary to begin with. It feels like anyone could become a king or queen in Narnia.<br><br>Anyway, obviously I'm massively biased about this book, because it's one of the first books I remember reading as a child, and I adored it then. I always used to read it when I was sick -- or if I was too sick to sit up, I'd watch the animated movie from my vantage-point of being draped over the sofa.<br><br>My mother always says that it was something of a toss-up whether Tolkien and C.S. Lewis created Middle-earth and Narnia, or whether it could've happened the other way round. (She also adds that she likes both, but would prefer Tolkien's, whichever one that happened to be.) I don't know about that, knowing about their religious and philosophical beliefs and how they differed, but I can very much see their influence on each other. </br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9982837784338603343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10643561025984330430">Love, love, love this book!!!<br><br>This is a re-read for me and it gets better every time I read it! I love how childhood innocence is at the core of this book and also the wonderful themes told through beautiful characters (people &amp; animals) and their struggles throughout the story. <br><br>The scene with Aslan and the mice chewing off his ropes is one of the most powerful and beautifully told parts in the story. Lucy is just adorable, Edmund is very relatable, and the simplicity of adding in the foresha</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText10643561025984330430" style="display:none">Love, love, love this book!!!<br><br>This is a re-read for me and it gets better every time I read it! I love how childhood innocence is at the core of this book and also the wonderful themes told through beautiful characters (people &amp; animals) and their struggles throughout the story. <br><br>The scene with Aslan and the mice chewing off his ropes is one of the most powerful and beautifully told parts in the story. Lucy is just adorable, Edmund is very relatable, and the simplicity of adding in the foreshadowing of the lamppost within the wintery trees gives me chills every time!  To say this book is brilliant is an understatement. It's children's literature at its best, because adults will love it too! </br></br></br></br></span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_39377171" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39377171" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny" title="Manny"><img alt="Manny" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1227275844p2/1713956.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39377171?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 05, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1713956-manny" title="Manny">Manny</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=children">children</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer39377171" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer9071168364990282401"><br>I read this book when I was 9 or 10, and I really enjoyed it. Like most kids that age, I didn't notice it was Christian propaganda... I just thought it was a great story. The bit about Aslan allowing himself to be killed, and then coming back to life, seemed, I don't know, <em>illogical</em>... was all this stuff about the Deep Magic and the Emperor over the Sea necessary? It didn't seem to add much to the plot. But the tear-drenched scene with Susan and Lucy keeping guard over Aslan's dead body was effe</br></span>
<span id="freeText9071168364990282401" style="display:none"><br>I read this book when I was 9 or 10, and I really enjoyed it. Like most kids that age, I didn't notice it was Christian propaganda... I just thought it was a great story. The bit about Aslan allowing himself to be killed, and then coming back to life, seemed, I don't know, <em>illogical</em>... was all this stuff about the Deep Magic and the Emperor over the Sea necessary? It didn't seem to add much to the plot. But the tear-drenched scene with Susan and Lucy keeping guard over Aslan's dead body was effective, as was the resurrection. And it felt good to see how surprised the Witch was when he unexpectedly turned up at the battle. So maybe it wasn't such a bad idea after all. <br><br>The book certainly had many strong points. Finding a magic doorway in a wardrobe was <em>cool</em>, as was the idea that time would pass at different rates in different worlds. I loved the preposterous tea-party at Mr. Tumnus's house. Even at age 9, I could see that the sardines and cake needed a lot of explanation, but it was funny. I had similar feelings about Mrs. Beaver's sewing machine and Father Christmas and his sack of presents. (Come on. How is hiding their breakfast tray in there?) The White Witch was a satisfactory baddie, as was Maugrim. And the Turkish Delight. <em>Everyone</em> enjoys the bit with the Turkish Delight. My own kids were fascinated by it when they were small, and disappointed when they tried real Turkish Delight and found it was nothing special.<br><br>I still sort of like the book, even though I now feel a bit annoyed that he is trying to manipulate me. But enough of the time he's forgetting to preach and just having fun letting the story go where it wants to go, so I forgive him. Maybe he did teach me some Christian virtues after all :)</br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9071168364990282401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeText1349475156979373402" style="display:none">I'm a bit too upset by the forced #2 position of what was and always will be, to me, the first book of the series, yeah yeah yeah I know what Lewis's position is on the subject. Let's say Lewis decides, later in his life, that he's a Mormon. He's shedding tears about all the preëxistence lost, calculating the tithe complete with compounded back interest, throwing out all his tea bags. He's been reborn, is working to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Restorationism" rel="nofollow">Restore</a>, and plans to rename himself C.LDS. Lewis. The whole 9 yards and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degrees_of_Glory" rel="nofollow">3 heavens</a>, capiche? And he declares that the <em>Voyage of the Dawn Treader</em> is now the <em>Voyage of the Rvnd. Brigham Young</em>, and they're sailing not to the End of the World but to Salt Lake City, and Repicheep takes all the merpeople as wives. What would you do? Accept it, make like Winston Smith and handle the order to <strong>narnchron 3 boatname <em>dawntreader</em> doubleplusungood refs unship rewrite fullwise upsub antefiling</strong>? Well, you can take your Narnia rewritten fullwise, and you can cram a 15-ton statue of Felix Edmundovich Dzerzhinsky right up your ass with walnuts, buddy.</span>

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer72937411" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer7015731278239009043">The  Pevensie children wander into a world where the truths of the universe are played out in the forms of Aslan and the White Witch. One of their number falls for a temptation that after he receives it, seems so very small for the price paid. Was it really the candy or something deeper? <br><br>This is a wonderful story whether you go to it for the deeper truths found here or just for the story itself.</br></br></span>
</span>

<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202312417" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1662951-jzhunagev" title="jzhunagev"><img alt="jzhunagev" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352945991p2/1662951.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202312417?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 07, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1662951-jzhunagev" title="jzhunagev">jzhunagev</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/6555529-the-lion-the-witch-and-the-wardrobe" title="Hardcover 9780061715051">review of another edition</a>
<div>
<span class="greyText">Recommended to jzhunagev by: </span>
      the "Voice"
  </div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=award-winning-best-seller">award-winning-best-seller</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=childrens-or-middle-grade-books">childrens-or-middle-grade-books</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=dark-chest-of-wonders">dark-chest-of-wonders</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=from-book-to-film">from-book-to-film</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662951-jzhunagev?shelf=religion-books">religion-books</a>,

<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer202312417" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer14002445390882617092"><blockquote>
<strong>The Land Beyond the Wardrobe</strong><br>(A Book Review of C. S. Lewis’s <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>)</br></blockquote><br><br>One is never too old to appreciate a good story; this can never be truer when I opened the pages of C. S. Lewis’s beloved classic, <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, inviting me in to the magical world of The Chronicles of Narnia.<br><br>Admittedly, the book was never introduced to me when I was a child or even in any of my high school reading. It is only when I watched Disney’s wonderful movie adaptation</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText14002445390882617092" style="display:none"><blockquote>
<strong>The Land Beyond the Wardrobe</strong><br>(A Book Review of C. S. Lewis’s <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>)</br></blockquote><br><br>One is never too old to appreciate a good story; this can never be truer when I opened the pages of C. S. Lewis’s beloved classic, <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, inviting me in to the magical world of The Chronicles of Narnia.<br><br>Admittedly, the book was never introduced to me when I was a child or even in any of my high school reading. It is only when I watched Disney’s wonderful movie adaptation where it came to my attention that it was based on a book. Still, it had taken a number of years before I had actually read it along with the whole series. Do I lament the fact that this was not a part of and came into my life way past my childhood or even when I’m too old (but not actually) to be called a teen? I believe a book chooses the reader when one is ready to receive it, and it comes in such a propitious time of one’s life.<br><br>Taken as it is one can easily enjoy <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> (from here on out referred to as LWW for convenience’s sake) as a simple adventure and fairy tale of brothers and sisters Peter, Susan, Edmund, and Lucy Pevensie who one day discovers a wardrobe that acts as a bridge into a mystical land called Narnia. Lewis populates this enchanted landscape with talking animals and creatures inspired from legend and mythology like fauns, minotaurs, dwarfs, centaurs, giants and Father Christmas, to name just a few, that adds to the delight of young and adult readers. At the heart of the book’s plot is a quest that concerns the Penvensies, a prophecy they must fulfill to right wrongs along with the coming of Aslan, king of kings, to bring an end to the hundred-year reign of the White Witch and her evil spell that placed Narnia in a never-ending winter.<br><br>Those above along with magic, treachery, sacrifice and the battle pitting Aslan’s army against the White Witch’s hordes are some of the elements of the story I strongly responded to when I saw the film and still are when I subsequently read the book. Yet other than conjuring a fantastic yarn for the enjoyment of the readers there is more than that meets the eye in LWW.<br><br>What I think draw the initiates and enthusiasts alike to Lewis’s masterpiece repeatedly is that LLW is more than its story, as one intending to dig deep within its plot can discover for themselves a treasure trove of lessons. Indeed, it incorporates Christian allusions and symbols that at first glance is already obvious—much to the chagrin of its adult readers—yet the author handles it with grace and he isn’t heavy handed with the message of his book for we are already disarmed by the charming voice of the narrator. As smooth as snow the story draws us in, so much so that the fluidity of Lewis’s underlying narrative manages to communicate in us gems of lessons in faith, reason, as well as moral truth with us being not quite aware of it at first.<br><br>As humans there is built in us a trait, whether we be aware of it or not, a deep-seated natural ability to determine truth on a subconscious and emotional level and be attracted to it. This I believe is what C. S. Lewis taps into, that’s why for generations now readers of <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> (and all of the books in <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>) are easily drawn it; that with Lewis’s pen we are not only touched by the aesthetics of a tale well told, it is to experience, too, the hand of the divine.<br><br>_________________________<br>Book Details: Book <strong>#39</strong> for 2011<br>Published by Harper Collins<br>(Hardcover, November 2009 First Printing)<br>176 pages<br>Read in:  September 03, 2011<br>My Rating: ★ ★ ★ ★ ★<br><br>[See this review on my book blog <a href="http://wp.me/p14wAA-nl" rel="nofollow">
<strong>Dark Chest of Wonders</strong>
</a> and for many others.]</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="14002445390882617092" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_198771305" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198771305" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5978617-bobby-luke" title="Bobby Luke"><img alt="Bobby Luke" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1313033510p2/5978617.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/198771305?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 09, 2011</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/5978617-bobby-luke" title="Bobby Luke">Bobby Luke</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer198771305" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer8483085095454853475">This is the first time I have read this book since waay back in elementary school, and although I haven't read many of the other books in the series, this book is definitely one of the best if not the best of the series. (I will let you know if I change my mind when I get through the rest of the series).  It never loses pace, and C.S. Lewis's comical style is always fun to read.  (Some of the side comments made me laugh out loud.)  He successfully manages to blend humor, adventure, mystery, and exc</span>
<span id="freeText8483085095454853475" style="display:none">This is the first time I have read this book since waay back in elementary school, and although I haven't read many of the other books in the series, this book is definitely one of the best if not the best of the series. (I will let you know if I change my mind when I get through the rest of the series).  It never loses pace, and C.S. Lewis's comical style is always fun to read.  (Some of the side comments made me laugh out loud.)  He successfully manages to blend humor, adventure, mystery, and excitement, all the while paralleling the most important event in all of mankind.  Interestingly enough, the entire parallel of The Atonement and The Resurrection lasts only 2 chapters.  My favorite part of the book is when Lucy and Susan follow Aslan, and then help him in his slow march to the stone table.  The emotion in the story and the significance of the parallel at this point in the story is magnificent.  Without rambling on and on, this book is everything a classic should be.  A must read for all fans of fantasy, and who consider themselves religious.</span>
<a data-text-id="8483085095454853475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis">About C.S. Lewis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
<a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis"><img alt="1069006" class="right" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1367519078p2/1069006.jpg"/></a>
<div class="readable">
<span id="freeTextContainer4086784393401507936">CLIVE STAPLES LEWIS (1898–1963) was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than th</span>
<span id="freeText4086784393401507936" style="display:none">CLIVE STAPLES LEWIS (1898–1963) was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than thirty books, allowing him to reach a vast audience, and his works continue to attract thousands of new readers every year. His most distinguished and popular accomplishments include <em>Mere Christianity</em>, <em>Out of the Silent Planet</em>, <em>The Great Divorce</em>, <em>The Screwtape Letters</em>, and the universally acknowledged classics <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>. To date, the Narnia books have sold over 100 million copies and been transformed into three major motion pictures.</span>
<a data-text-id="4086784393401507936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/4790821-the-lion-the-witch-and-the-wardrobe">Quotes from The Lion, the Witch an...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
<div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
<span class="readable">“I hope no one who reads this book has been quite as miserable as Susan and Lucy were that night; but if you have been - if you've been up all night and cried till you have no more tears left in you - you will know that there comes in the end a sort of quietness. You feel as if nothing is ever going to happen again.”</span>
<nobr>
            —
            <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/143080-i-hope-no-one-who-reads-this-book-has-been">694 likes</a>
</nobr>
<div class="clear"></div>
</div>
<div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
<span class="readable">“I wrote this story for you, but when I began it I had not realized that girls grow quicker than books. As a result you are already too old for fairy tales, and by the time it is printed and bound you will be older still. But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again. You can then take it down from some upper shelf, dust it, and tell me what you think of it. I shall probably be too deaf to hear, and too old to understand a word you say, but I shall still be your affectionate Godfather, C. S. Lewis.”</span>
<nobr>
            —
            <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/56726-i-wrote-this-story-for-you-but-when-i-began">599 likes</a>
</nobr>
<div class="clear"></div>
